    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer13712451393042230453">Michael Cunningham’s celebrated novel is the story of two boyhood friends: Jonathan, lonely, introspective, and unsure of himself; and Bobby, hip, dark, and inarticulate. In New York after college, Bobby moves in with Jonathan and his roommate, Clare, a veteran of the city's erotic wars. Bobby and Clare fall in love, scuttling the plans of Jonathan, who is gay, to father C</span>
  <span id="freeText13712451393042230453" style="display:none">Michael Cunningham’s celebrated novel is the story of two boyhood friends: Jonathan, lonely, introspective, and unsure of himself; and Bobby, hip, dark, and inarticulate. In New York after college, Bobby moves in with Jonathan and his roommate, Clare, a veteran of the city's erotic wars. Bobby and Clare fall in love, scuttling the plans of Jonathan, who is gay, to father Clare's child. Then, when Clare and Bobby have a baby, the three move to a small house upstate to raise "their" child together and, with an odd friend, Alice, create a new kind of family. <i>A Home at the End of the World</i> masterfully depicts the charged, fragile relationships of urban life today.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13712451393042230453" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2764126503" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2764126503" />
    <a title="Violet wells" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells"><img alt="Violet wells" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442260743p2/4196681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2764126503?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Violet wells" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Violet wells" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells">Violet wells</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2764126503" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6904750946261776801">Perhaps one reason Shakespeare is so untouchably brilliant is that you have no idea who he is from his work. This is rarely true of novelists. Read a Fitzgerald or Hemmingway novel and there's the author himself on almost every page. No one doubts Dr Zhivago is Pasternak himself. And I could carry on with innumerable other examples. True some novelists are more elusive in their work and demand more detective work, like Henry James or Nabokov or Virginia Woolf. But when you read a number of novel</span>
  <span id="freeText6904750946261776801" style="display:none">Perhaps one reason Shakespeare is so untouchably brilliant is that you have no idea who he is from his work. This is rarely true of novelists. Read a Fitzgerald or Hemmingway novel and there's the author himself on almost every page. No one doubts Dr Zhivago is Pasternak himself. And I could carry on with innumerable other examples. True some novelists are more elusive in their work and demand more detective work, like Henry James or Nabokov or Virginia Woolf. But when you read a number of novels by the same author you begin to recognise the same pivot and cast of characters popping up over and over again. Even Shakespeare's characters reappear in his plays. One of the fascinating things about reading several books by the same author is this growing familiarity with the limited scope of his/her world. Which is why there's often a sense that every author is telling variations on the same story over and over again. The conclusion perhaps is that in life only a handful of individuals have an impact on the formation of character and the way we see the world. Virginia Woolf's point of departure in The Waves. I've now read two Cunningham novels and started a third and have begun to feel that not only do I know what to expect from his created worlds but also that I know a lot about him, the person behind the text. In much the same way we have all built notions about the real life identity of our fellow reviewers on this site. Notions which, of course, might be miles off the mark. It's always a slightly unreal feeling to feel we know someone we've never met.<br /> <br />To the novel itself. I would have edited out the first chapter of The Hours. Here I would have edited out the last chapter. The penultimate chapter is a miles better ending. That said, this novel left me wanting to know what happens to the characters after the curtain comes down. Is that though a good sign? On the one hand it's a testament to how well he had me invested in them. On the other I felt he curtailed the story just as it was becoming even more compelling. As if Ferrante had ended Elena and Lina's story with My Brilliant Friend, a decent novel but hardly a masterpiece without its other three parts. <br /><br />A Home at the End of the World is about an unconventional living arrangement. Two men, one gay, the other so pliant he'll try anything and an older woman. There are lulls but loads of great stuff about parenting and romantic expectation and social conditioning and on the whole it's beautifully written and thoroughly engaging.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6904750946261776801" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <a title="Lee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/103291-lee"><img alt="Lee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1180536838p2/103291.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1546654?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee" href="/user/show/103291-lee">Lee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312202316" href="/book/show/386242.A_Home_at_the_End_of_the_World">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1546654" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9346695298100862944">This book was my introduction to Michael Cunningham, and when I finished it I cried. And then went out and bought everything he'd ever written. <br/><br/>I fell in love with this book. At that time in my life I could relate to its characters and their story in a unique way, but it was also Cunningham's writing: spare, lovely, gorgeously aware of minutiae, devastatingly honest. There is a sadness in his work that fills me with a profound loneliness that I find myself both overwhelmed by and grateful for.<br/><br/>"A</span>
  <span id="freeText9346695298100862944" style="display:none">This book was my introduction to Michael Cunningham, and when I finished it I cried. And then went out and bought everything he'd ever written. <br /><br />I fell in love with this book. At that time in my life I could relate to its characters and their story in a unique way, but it was also Cunningham's writing: spare, lovely, gorgeously aware of minutiae, devastatingly honest. There is a sadness in his work that fills me with a profound loneliness that I find myself both overwhelmed by and grateful for.<br /><br />"A Home at the End of the World" tells the story of Jonathan and Bobby, friends since their childhood in Cleveland. Thanks to various family tragedies, Bobby is damaged and strange. Jonathan, raised by a loving family, is naive and kind. The boys become friends, as close as brothers, but the friendship is quickly complicated by the their muddled teenaged libidos. They begin to experiment together sexually.<br /><br />Embarrassed and confused, they lose touch for years only to reunite in New York. Jonathan is openly gay and living with Clare, an older, quirky single woman. Bobby is still lost, simply wandering. Clare and Jonathan, in the classic gay-man-straight-woman pact, have already agreed to have a baby together, but Clare and Bobby immediately become lovers and Clare quickly becomes pregnant. Pact off. Or so it seems, until the group manages to cobble together a little family and a life of the most patchwork variety.<br /><br />It's become, in this "Will &amp; Grace" era, a familiar story. But what Cunningham does with the characters is stunning. Bobby's rootlessness and desperate pansexuality in lieu of true love and nurturing is haunting. Jonathan's desire for a "traditional" life (kids, home, security) and the sadness that comes when he sees that life slipping away is heartbreaking. Clare's mix of selfishness and determined independence is compelling. Cunningham manages, beautifully, to shed new light on the old questions: how do we find home and how are we best loved? The answer, according to "A Home at the End of the World" is a refreshing one: we create it ourselves.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9346695298100862944" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/45403561" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/45403561?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer45403561" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2648269067577812219">This is the "Less than Zero"ish novel of the popular NYC writer, and just like Bret Easton Ellis' depiction of the derelict children of sunny Cali in the 80's, Cunningham encapsulates the latter 80's in the East Village & early 60's, 70's in the stark midwest) with lost souls and unique individuals.<br/><br/>The plot is this: Two guys and a gal play house together because they are (equally?) in love.<br/><br/>Obviously there is more to it, as it differs somewhat from the pretty damn good movie with an additional ch</span>
  <span id="freeText2648269067577812219" style="display:none">This is the "Less than Zero"ish novel of the popular NYC writer, and just like Bret Easton Ellis' depiction of the derelict children of sunny Cali in the 80's, Cunningham encapsulates the latter 80's in the East Village &amp; early 60's, 70's in the stark midwest) with lost souls and unique individuals.<br /><br />The plot is this: Two guys and a gal play house together because they are (equally?) in love.<br /><br />Obviously there is more to it, as it differs somewhat from the pretty damn good movie with an additional character Erich (he, not Jonathan, gets AIDS!) &amp; a fourth point of view: that of Alice, the pot smoking desperate housewife (later widow).<br /><br />This episodic drama is written carefully, it seems, and it sometimes drifts into insane allusions (see: The Hours) while keeping the normalcy so entertaining to behold. The reader mirrors Bobby in that ethereal aspect... one is not alive nor dead following the triplets around. One just IS.<br /><br />Like I said, this one is an early example of a genius master. My favorite Cunningham is "Specimen Days" which sounds, and is (spectacularly) weird. Here he tries to get something right, and thoroughly succeeds. I like that he is taking another direction currently however, even after all the fame and acclaim garnered by "The Hours". Cunningham is ambitious, and I am yet to be disappointed, though I probably never will*. <br /><br />* False. See: The Snow Queen</span>
  <a data-text-id="2648269067577812219" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6644472-a-home-at-the-end-of-the-world">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1098997349" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14970825004324778650"><br/><br/><br/><i>
  <u>Directly after I've finished the book:</u>
</i><br/><br/><b>THAT, my friends, is an excellent example of a literary fiction.</b> <br/><br/>And it is not easy to rate the books of the genre. <br/>They could be everything - from 2 to 5 stars. <br/><br/>Well, I have to decide between 4 or 5 in this specific case, but it won't be easier to write a review for it. The reason WHY I love literary fiction-it makes you not only feel, but think, think a lot. <br/>Oh, yes, it can even detect our hidden individual talent for philosophy.<br/> <br/>Now I'll go into my tu</span>
  <span id="freeText14970825004324778650" style="display:none"><br><br><br><i>
  <u>Directly after I've finished the book:</u>
</i><br><br><b>THAT, my friends, is an excellent example of a literary fiction.</b> <br><br>And it is not easy to rate the books of the genre. <br>They could be everything - from 2 to 5 stars. <br><br>Well, I have to decide between 4 or 5 in this specific case, but it won't be easier to write a review for it. The reason WHY I love literary fiction-it makes you not only feel, but think, think a lot. <br>Oh, yes, it can even detect our hidden individual talent for philosophy.<br> <br>Now I'll go into my tub to think about...the fragility of life, the variety of love, the power of human bonding, the senseless of death, the significance of the moment and the solemnity of words...<br><br><i>
  <u>I decided not to hide my review within the spoiler tags=&gt;spoiler-free</u>
</i>:<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1417028791i/12171682.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Michael Cunningham</b> is an extraordinary story teller. I was drawn into this multifaceted journey across years and cities from the very first page. I re-read some lines again and again letting the words slowly grow on me before turning to the next page. The writing is rich and compelling. I highlighted the half of the book. <br><br>The author takes us through the lives of the three main characters- Bobby, Jonathan and Clare- their childhood, families, their loves, lives, dreams, disappointments, losses, small triumphs, big changes, ups and downs. The trio would find themselves becoming major parts of each others lives. The relationships between these three characters between the late 70s and the early 80s are the core of the story.<br><br>This book is about friendship and family bond. And it is about love, that is so different in all its facets and appearances. <br>There is no rules for love, it is genderless and ageless, and timeless.<br><br>And because all my dear friends wanted to know if there is a HEA:<br>It is a very realistic ending, full of hope and <i>mererly present</i> and at the same it is an open ending - Michael Cunningham trusts his readers to draw the right conclusions about his characters - and this ending is melancholic, poignant and simply BEAUTIFUL.<br><br><br><i>My only complaint, a very tiny one - the multiple first person's POV. I would have given it 5 stars if not every character had his own first person's POV. In spite of the fact that this kind of telling is my favourite, I think the book was a bit overloaded with it. As the result -all these different POVs sounded very similar at the end. <br>It was always ONLY the voice of Michael Cunningham behind.</i> <br><br> <br><b>Highly recommended!</b><br><br><br><i>P.S. I'd like to thank Nick for a nice exchange of opinions about the book. It is this kind of books you NEED to talk about while/after reading it. It is a kind of book you shouldn't read alone. <br>It is why I love my GR friends so much. </i></span>

<div id="review_2195560" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2195560" />
    <a title="Taylor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32865-taylor"><img alt="Taylor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567607577p2/32865.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2195560?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Taylor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Taylor" href="/user/show/32865-taylor">Taylor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312202316" href="/book/show/386242.A_Home_at_the_End_of_the_World">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=made-for-the-screen">made-for-the-screen</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=not-my-thing">not-my-thing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=lgbtqa">lgbtqa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=the-power-of-love">the-power-of-love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=country-mouse">country-mouse</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2195560" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11600097694137787147">I only sort of liked this, so I honestly don't have too much to say about it. It wasn't remarkable, but it wasn't awful.<br/><br/>It basically follows a set of three friends - one women, two men - and examines their relationships, both with each other, and with people from the outside world (mothers, fathers, girlfriends, boyfriends).<br/><br/>It reads almost a little blandly. I suppose you could say it's more of a character study and less about the plot. But then I couldn't say that it was very successful, because</span>
  <span id="freeText11600097694137787147" style="display:none">I only sort of liked this, so I honestly don't have too much to say about it. It wasn't remarkable, but it wasn't awful.<br /><br />It basically follows a set of three friends - one women, two men - and examines their relationships, both with each other, and with people from the outside world (mothers, fathers, girlfriends, boyfriends).<br /><br />It reads almost a little blandly. I suppose you could say it's more of a character study and less about the plot. But then I couldn't say that it was very successful, because I didn't feel connected to any of the characters. They had their moments, but the things they felt and experienced weren't enough to move me emotionally - and I'm a pretty big sap. Even this was a little too sappy and unnecessarily dramatic for me. There's not a lot of joy in this book, so after awhile it feels like the characters are just holding on to each other out of history and obligation. They seem almost afraid to meet new people. Or maybe just lazy.<br /><br />If you're determined to read it, borrow it. Or buy it used. But if you want a better examination of the dynamic between two men and one woman, read <i>The Adventures of Kavalier &amp; Clay</i>.<br /><br />Also, has anyone read any of Cunningham's other works? I kind of want to read <i>The Hours</i>, but this makes me a little wary.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11600097694137787147" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer108730879" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13463873894516035837">"You don't necessarily meet a lot of people in this world."<br/><br/>This is the first of Michael Cunningham's books I've read, but I will be reading all of them. He just flat gets it. By the time I was halfway through, I more or less disliked two of the three main characters, but I wasn't tired of reading about them. I wanted to figure them out. I wanted to like them and if I didn't, I wanted to understand why. <br/><br/>This is one of those books that you read a sentence or a paragraph or a scene and it hits you </span>
  <span id="freeText13463873894516035837" style="display:none">"You don't necessarily meet a lot of people in this world."<br /><br />This is the first of Michael Cunningham's books I've read, but I will be reading all of them. He just flat gets it. By the time I was halfway through, I more or less disliked two of the three main characters, but I wasn't tired of reading about them. I wanted to figure them out. I wanted to like them and if I didn't, I wanted to understand why. <br /><br />This is one of those books that you read a sentence or a paragraph or a scene and it hits you deep down, sometimes in the places where you're most insecure. (If you're someone who underlines quotations, get new pencils. Get a *box* of pencils.) There were times when I was sad or upset about something and would read another book instead because I didn't want to feel everything that this one brought up.<br /><br />I'm making this book sound like a big downer. It isn't. It's exhilarating, like all the best books, because it tells you what you know is true and then makes you look at it all again.</span>

<div id="review_1727924" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1727924" />
    <a title="Punk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/116652-punk"><img alt="Punk" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1181025939p2/116652.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1727924?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Punk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Punk" href="/user/show/116652-punk">Punk</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/116652-punk?shelf=queer">queer</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1727924" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer539309312616419908">Fiction. This is the story of Bobby and Jonathan -- best friends, almost brothers, almost in love -- how they grow up together, how they grow apart, how they meet Clare, and how they all try to make a home together. It sounds cozy -- I love self-made families -- but this is an exceedingly lonely book. No one's able to make any lasting connections and everyone's alone in one way or another. It's sad, but written so well. Cunningham has an easy way with language; his prose is simple and honest, wi</span>
  <span id="freeText539309312616419908" style="display:none">Fiction. This is the story of Bobby and Jonathan -- best friends, almost brothers, almost in love -- how they grow up together, how they grow apart, how they meet Clare, and how they all try to make a home together. It sounds cozy -- I love self-made families -- but this is an exceedingly lonely book. No one's able to make any lasting connections and everyone's alone in one way or another. It's sad, but written so well. Cunningham has an easy way with language; his prose is simple and honest, with wonderful spots of color and noise: <i>If Bobby moved with the methodical, slightly bovine will of a vacuum cleaner, sucking up each errand and task, Jonathan clattered along like an eggbeater.</i> That's Clare speaking -- several characters take turns narrating -- and you can sense her resentment even through the humor. Cunningham's prose is delightful, but cutting. As is this book.<br /><br />Three stars for affecting, sympathetic characters and Cunningham's effortlessly interesting prose. </span>
  <a data-text-id="539309312616419908" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lukas Anthony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lukas Anthony" href="/user/show/10117401-lukas-anthony">Lukas Anthony</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10117401-lukas-anthony?shelf=has-some-gay">has-some-gay</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1887462176" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15592460335716761793"><b>A couple of jumbled thoughts...</b><br/><br/>I found this to be quite an introspective character focused novel. It's plot is sort used as a backdrop to the characters working through their insecurities about relationships, loneliness, and the expectations they have about their life. Not a lot happens plot wise really, it's more about what is going on within the heads of the characters. <br/>Sometimes I was completely enthralled with them. The first half is especially engaging with the dynamic between the boys and </span>
  <span id="freeText15592460335716761793" style="display:none"><b>A couple of jumbled thoughts...</b><br /><br />I found this to be quite an introspective character focused novel. It's plot is sort used as a backdrop to the characters working through their insecurities about relationships, loneliness, and the expectations they have about their life. Not a lot happens plot wise really, it's more about what is going on within the heads of the characters. <br />Sometimes I was completely enthralled with them. The first half is especially engaging with the dynamic between the boys and Johnathan's mum, but I will admit it lost be a bit with the introduction of Clare, she never truly felt like a real character to me.<br />I also didn't like the focus one character took towards the end, it felt a bit more like introducing an 'issue' instead of the character feeling organic.<br /><br />Ultimately, while this book seems to sell itself on it's unconventional relationship, to me it was more about a fear of loneliness and them trying to fix that. If I was to give this a book another title it would be <i>'Being lonely with other people'.</i></span>

          </span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/617334-madeline?shelf=queer">queer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/617334-madeline?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/617334-madeline?shelf=depression">depression</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/617334-madeline?shelf=marriage-lets-you-down">marriage-lets-you-down</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/617334-madeline?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/617334-madeline?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/617334-madeline?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/617334-madeline?shelf=music">music</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer144679532" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14832089469958002652">Well - there are a couple of really good lines in this book, but they are almost invariably followed by something truly horrible and trite, expanding upon the theme and beating it into your head. So the prose is overwritten and labored (and sometimes mannered and affected), but as a reader of Victorian literature, I could probably forgive that - Hardy has committed greater sins, and I have read and enjoyed those sins as committed by by lesser writers than Hardy - but the <i>problem</i> with <i>A Home at t</i></span>
  <span id="freeText14832089469958002652" style="display:none">Well - there are a couple of really good lines in this book, but they are almost invariably followed by something truly horrible and trite, expanding upon the theme and beating it into your head. So the prose is overwritten and labored (and sometimes mannered and affected), but as a reader of Victorian literature, I could probably forgive that - Hardy has committed greater sins, and I have read and enjoyed those sins as committed by by lesser writers than Hardy - but the <i>problem</i> with <i>A Home at the End of the World</i> is that it never feels authentic: particularly, the characters. They are all a collection of surfaces; Cunningham constantly explains them to us, but does little to justify his explanations, and their supposed virtues just make them cowards.* Also, although it is a novel in alternating first person chapters, none of them have unique voices - they all sound like each other, there are no psychological/verbal idiosyncrasies. You want something more nuanced and developed from a novel like this - and something more challenging, too. I think the general idea that "these people are too afraid of the world and themselves to be part of it" is a good one, if perhaps more suited to short fiction than a novel, except I'm not 100% sure that the novel actually takes this position: there's a nasty undercurrent of sanctimony.<br /><br />Music is supposed to be a big part of their lives, but it never feels that way - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2929.Nick_Hornby" title="Nick Hornby" rel="nofollow noopener">Nick Hornby</a>, among others, has done that better. And food is also supposed to be important. Those are two rich thematic elements, but they just <i>lie there</i> on the page, like gestures at depth and sensual experience. It sort of gave me second-hand embarrassment.<br /><br />* Like, if you want to read that, Hemingway, Wharton, and Fitzgerald are your go-to writers, right? And I say this as someone who doesn't even like Hemingway.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14832089469958002652" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24500926-lea?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24500926-lea?shelf=read-in-english">read-in-english</a>,

<span id="freeTextContainer16490664700803772919">4.5/5 <br/><br/>This started out amazing, got kind of muddled for me in the middle and ended brilliantly. This is about a kind of love triangle between Jonathan, his best friend Bobby and Clare. <br/><br/>Jonathan and Bobby meet as teenagers and become inseperable. While Jonathan is gay, it is less clear what Bobby is, besides devoted to Jonathan. When Jonathan moves away to New York City (this is a Cunningham novel, so of course there's NYC... and complicated sibling relationships and some drugs, but not that many</span>
  <span id="freeText16490664700803772919" style="display:none">4.5/5 <br /><br />This started out amazing, got kind of muddled for me in the middle and ended brilliantly. This is about a kind of love triangle between Jonathan, his best friend Bobby and Clare. <br /><br />Jonathan and Bobby meet as teenagers and become inseperable. While Jonathan is gay, it is less clear what Bobby is, besides devoted to Jonathan. When Jonathan moves away to New York City (this is a Cunningham novel, so of course there's NYC... and complicated sibling relationships and some drugs, but not that many), he meets Clare. They, platonically, love each other. When Bobby moves to the city, he and Clare also fall in love - less platonically. And then Clare gets pregnant. <br /><br />I don't want to spoil the story, but I can just say, that this novel isn't great because of the story itself but because of the wonderful writing style and its characters. This is also why I can't give the book 5 stars: Clare just never really clicked for me, especially in the chapters from her point of view. Was I not supposed to like her? Was I supposed to see this, ultimatively, as a love story between Jonathan and Bobby? I'm not sure. I did like the chapters from Jonathan's mother Alice, but I do always find it harder to relate to Cunningham's female characters and I don't think that's a coincidence. They just don't feel real, which is especially bad in novels that are so character-driven. <br /><br />Despite this, I really enjoyed the book overall.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16490664700803772919" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_612054534" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/612054534" />
    <a title="Jennifer Ochoa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17138550-jennifer-ochoa"><img alt="Jennifer Ochoa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395509673p2/17138550.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/612054534?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer Ochoa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer Ochoa" href="/user/show/17138550-jennifer-ochoa">Jennifer Ochoa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6644472-a-home-at-the-end-of-the-world">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17138550-jennifer-ochoa?shelf=read-ebooks-owned">read-ebooks-owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17138550-jennifer-ochoa?shelf=2015">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer612054534" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18173363997248794326">I think I'm experiencing Cunningham Fatigue. I've read four of his novels in the last two years and they are starting to run together. He does seem to work with very similar themes in his works, something I actually like about him. <br/><br/>This novel reminds me a lot of his most recent novel, The Snow Queen, another story about a trio, quartet if you want to count Alice in this novel, and Liz in the other novel. (Liz is very similar to Clare, I should add). It feels like Cunningham uses his novels to wo</span>
  <span id="freeText18173363997248794326" style="display:none">I think I'm experiencing Cunningham Fatigue. I've read four of his novels in the last two years and they are starting to run together. He does seem to work with very similar themes in his works, something I actually like about him. <br /><br />This novel reminds me a lot of his most recent novel, The Snow Queen, another story about a trio, quartet if you want to count Alice in this novel, and Liz in the other novel. (Liz is very similar to Clare, I should add). It feels like Cunningham uses his novels to work through personal experiences, like maybe there is a Clare/Liz person in his life, maybe he had a Bobby/Tyler brother figure. I know the unhappy housewife character that occurs in this novel, The Hours, and Flesh and Blood is at least partially based on his mother, who died of cancer a few years after The Hours was published. It is no wonder that The Snow Queen concerns itself with a young woman dying of cancer, and not the unhappy housewife of earlier novels. It's a beautiful thing to see an author work through the difficulties of his life through his work. And it never fails to amaze me what a light and beautiful touch he has when creating his characters. <br /><br />So back to this novel. It takes place in the 1980's and centers around two childhood friends (Jonathan and Bobby) who enter into a complicated romantic threesome with a woman (Clare). One could call it a polyamourous relationship, but it's a bit more complex than that. Sex hardly factors in the equation for these three. It's more about creating the family they each want. <br /><br />Each of the three characters narrate chapters in the book, with Jonathan's mother Alice (the unhappy housewife) narrating some chapters as well. Alice's POV is mostly in the beginning, when Jonathan and Bobby were kids. Clare shows up a bit later, taking over as the central female in the boys' lives. Naturally the two women do not care for each other when they do meet :-)<br /><br />The characters are pretty good, especially Bobby, whose tragic losses, implied asexuality, and relentless yearning for a family make him the most interesting character in the book. I felt Jonathan and Clare lacked something in comparison. To go back to my Snow Queen comparison, the Barrett and Liz characters to which they resemble were more fleshed out. <br /><br />The story-line felt a little dragged out after awhile. There are only so many pages of relationship agony one can deal with before you lose interest and empathy. The beginning of the novel was superior in that regard. The tension between Jonathan, Bobby, and Alice was more exciting than the rather mundane tension between Jonathan, Bobby, and Clare, even with their "unusual" relationship configuration. <br /><br />The final pages of the book didn't work for me either. Cunningham ended his other works with such poetry, that I found myself in one of those "That's it?" moments when I turned the page to find the Acknowledgements statement. <br /><br />Not a bad read, especially if you love Cunningham, but I'd recommend The Snow Queen before this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18173363997248794326" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_202202468" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/202202468" />
    <a title="Juushika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/616321-juushika"><img alt="Juushika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541586729p2/616321.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/202202468?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Juushika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Juushika" href="/user/show/616321-juushika">Juushika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/616321-juushika?shelf=status-borrowed">status-borrowed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer202202468" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8631989371476551156">The story of a relationship between two childhood friends and a woman who enters their lives in adulthood, <i>A Home at the End of the World</i> is difficult to summarize because its plot is wide, rambling, and only half the point. Meandering from the childhood deaths that leave Bobby bereft, distant, and desperate for connection, to Jonathan's burgeoning sexuality and his fixation on Bobby, to the entrance of world-weary Clare and the fragile three-way relationship that forms on the basis of the share</span>
  <span id="freeText8631989371476551156" style="display:none">The story of a relationship between two childhood friends and a woman who enters their lives in adulthood, <i>A Home at the End of the World</i> is difficult to summarize because its plot is wide, rambling, and only half the point. Meandering from the childhood deaths that leave Bobby bereft, distant, and desperate for connection, to Jonathan's burgeoning sexuality and his fixation on Bobby, to the entrance of world-weary Clare and the fragile three-way relationship that forms on the basis of the shared love--but disparate sexuality--between them, the book offers constant forward motion but few concrete goals. Its goal, instead, is home--and this abstract concept creates a compelling story. The book's chapter-by-chapter headhopping is its weak point: each character has a strong external voice, but their interior monologues are near identical and unconvincing as a result. But in all other ways, Cunningham's portrayals are unflinching and precise. His characters are mundane and eccentric, intimately familiar and occasionally unlikable; the complex relationships between them are fueled by intense love but are never idealized or static. Cunningham offers moments of startling clarity, clear and sharp and painful as glass, into what it is to be a member of a family, an isolated individual connected to others in the attempt to build a home. <i>A Home at the End of the World</i> walks a delicate line between heartwarming and heartbreaking, and it's easy to oversell--but there is little excessive or maudlin about it, despite its brush against tragedies like the AIDS epidemic. It tempers its emotions with the discomforting mundanity of reality, and finds no easy or stable answers. If not for its moments of beauty, it would be depressing; instead, it's both and more: intimate, quiet, and compelling; a dream set within the realities of life; poignant, beautiful, painful; above all, real. It's not a book I can say I simply "enjoyed," but it is one I'm thankful to have read, and I recommend it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8631989371476551156" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8015255-giedre?shelf=1990s">1990s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8015255-giedre?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8015255-giedre?shelf=lgbt">lgbt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8015255-giedre?shelf=american">american</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer900140164" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14372956156005138548">"A Home at the End of the World" was my first Cunningham's book and I can already say that it's not going to be the last one. <br/><br/>The book is evolving slowly, and my reading was slowed down even more, because I was traveling while I was reading it. I am glad about it, as it seemed to require time to think it over and digest it. <br/><br/>The first thing that comes to mind when I think about "A Home at the End of the World" is that it is an awkward book. It is awkward, because it is too real, too intimate, too</span>
  <span id="freeText14372956156005138548" style="display:none">"A Home at the End of the World" was my first Cunningham's book and I can already say that it's not going to be the last one. <br /><br />The book is evolving slowly, and my reading was slowed down even more, because I was traveling while I was reading it. I am glad about it, as it seemed to require time to think it over and digest it. <br /><br />The first thing that comes to mind when I think about "A Home at the End of the World" is that it is an awkward book. It is awkward, because it is too real, too intimate, too raw. It does not try to sell us any ilusions about relationships, love or future. Just the opposite, while reading it, I felt thrown into all the complexity of it and in some way it stayed with me even after I finished the book.<br /><br />The book speaks in four different voices and takes the reader through a long period of lives of the four characters. I found the style and the frequent change of voices confusing at times, but at the same time I enjoyed the opportunity it gave me to live inside the different characters and understand them in a deeper and more complete way. <br /><br />The four characters, childhood friends Jonathan and Bobby, their flatmate, lover and companion Clare and Jonathan's mother Alice struggle with accepting and understanding the difference between the expectations of their significant others and society, their own idealized visions of what life, home and family is, and the reality. The book is breaking stereotypes and boundaries, provoking us to question conventional wisdom and inviting us to look for our own personal answers to the big questions posed by life.<br /><br />I am really happy I embarked upon this awkward journey and learned from seeing my own reflection in the different voices of the book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14372956156005138548" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2944537730" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17647511162477685074">Cunningham’s writing is, as always, powerful in its restraint. He is able, somehow, to create characters and settings that seem fully-formed and three-dimensional in such a way that one doesn’t notice them being realized until they already exist in one’s head, and heart. <br/>The House at the end of the World isn’t quite as affecting, or accomplished as Flesh & Blood or as stylistically impressive as The Hours, but nonetheless, I found myself drawn in, regardless. I wouldn’t advise this novel as an i</span>
  <span id="freeText17647511162477685074" style="display:none">Cunningham’s writing is, as always, powerful in its restraint. He is able, somehow, to create characters and settings that seem fully-formed and three-dimensional in such a way that one doesn’t notice them being realized until they already exist in one’s head, and heart. <br />The House at the end of the World isn’t quite as affecting, or accomplished as Flesh &amp; Blood or as stylistically impressive as The Hours, but nonetheless, I found myself drawn in, regardless. I wouldn’t advise this novel as an introduction to Cunningham’s writing, but do pick it up if you’re an established reader of his. I say so because I feel this novel offers more of a concept - that love can exist in myriad ways, despite what society might tell us - than a glimpse into a rich and full world. Despite that, it is a heartwarming, harrowing, and warm tale of love, in a multitude of forms, that I would implore you to pick up and devour at your first opportunity. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17647511162477685074" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer4914017388304640217">I really fucking liked this. It has lots of my favorite things in a novel: New York, suburban malaise, love, the 80s, parenting. It is the story of two boys and their families. It follows them into their adulthood where they meet the third character, Clare, and fall in love with her. The chapters are narrated by a different character, which can be annoying. I think Cunningham pulls it off. Each time a chapter started I would think 'oh good this character is really my favorite'.   <span id="freeText4914017388304640217" style="display:none">I really fucking liked this. It has lots of my favorite things in a novel: New York, suburban malaise, love, the 80s, parenting. It is the story of two boys and their families. It follows them into their adulthood where they meet the third character, Clare, and fall in love with her. The chapters are narrated by a different character, which can be annoying. I think Cunningham pulls it off. Each time a chapter started I would think 'oh good this character is really my favorite'. I loved Alice's chapters near the beginning. Like the bit where she says something like- 'and then I left the room as full of him as I had been when I was pregnant'. I did have some disappointments and questions at then end of the book, was Alice happy? how did Jonathan feel about how things turned out? and most of all, Why did Clare do what she did? I wish she hadn't, I don't think it was the right thing to do. Sometimes the intensity of emotion in this book was a bit too full on. Then again that's actually a really good thing right?</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3833380348305081481">I think Michael Cunningham's <i>The Hours</i> is an almost perfect work of fiction, and I have liked some of his other stories as well, but I'm starting to get a bit sick of the 'tragic gay' theme that runs throughout them all. I know that the early days of AIDS were horrific, with huge swathes of the gay community being wiped out, but I'm also tired of a great deal of gay fiction feeling that it has to be tragedy to gain kudos. All these books have helpless and hopeless outcomes for the gay characters</span>
  <span id="freeText3833380348305081481" style="display:none">I think Michael Cunningham's <i>The Hours</i> is an almost perfect work of fiction, and I have liked some of his other stories as well, but I'm starting to get a bit sick of the 'tragic gay' theme that runs throughout them all. I know that the early days of AIDS were horrific, with huge swathes of the gay community being wiped out, but I'm also tired of a great deal of gay fiction feeling that it has to be tragedy to gain kudos. All these books have helpless and hopeless outcomes for the gay characters, while the straight ones learn VALUABLE LIFE LESSONS from their death/upcoming death. Even books by gay authors seem to get centred around the straight characters and how awful AIDS is for them as their gay friends die. I don't even know how to end this review. I just don't even.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2966251580" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer13450664892463593943">If I could give this more than five stars, I would. It’s so beautiful and touching. The characters feel very real and it’s great that the story is told from more than one perspective. It has been a wonderful reading experience.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_688347571" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/688347571" />
    <a title="Salathiel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20955973-salathiel"><img alt="Salathiel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1527279811p2/20955973.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/688347571?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Salathiel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Salathiel" href="/user/show/20955973-salathiel">Salathiel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer688347571" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6902170930234242718"><i>A Home at the End of The World </i> is a love story. A convoluted, unbalanced, discombobulated love story, but a love story nonetheless.<br/> <br/>Jonathan meets Bobby in the eighth grade, and to call what forms between them a simple friendship would be to apply a cheap misnomer. They bond over weed, music, angst and rebellion. They discover physical sex together. They become defined by the other, a pair united by some commensal inner turmoil that seems incapable to define. And then they graduate high school,</span>
  <span id="freeText6902170930234242718" style="display:none"><i>A Home at the End of The World </i> is a love story. A convoluted, unbalanced, discombobulated love story, but a love story nonetheless.<br /> <br />Jonathan meets Bobby in the eighth grade, and to call what forms between them a simple friendship would be to apply a cheap misnomer. They bond over weed, music, angst and rebellion. They discover physical sex together. They become defined by the other, a pair united by some commensal inner turmoil that seems incapable to define. And then they graduate high school, and what once seemed inseparable, separates.<br /><br />I love novels where I am privileged to see the character grow, both in age and in substance. Bobby and Jonathan grow, morphing into versions of themselves that I didn’t see coming. One becomes otherworldly, approaching life and disaster with a calm that is perturbing. The other becomes frenetic, a ball of energy and movement that seems poised for a prison break. Both are thrilling and interesting in ways that carry the novel, though neither become what I, the lowly reader, would have foresaw.<br /><br />And perhaps that is why I loved this book, because it so aptly captured life’s unpredictability. We all start off young and impassioned; some slothful, some ambitious, some timid, some bold. We navigate our way from grade to high school, and then we are unleashed to the world. Or better yet, the world is unleashed on us. We think we are ready, only to find that differential equations and foreign language verb conjugations have little to do with solving the more pressing problems of life. Problems such as love, acceptance, actualization, and the ever niggling question of purpose. And it is often these problems and our response to them that determine who we become, with the wisdom gleaned from mama’s table no longer being the sole measuring stick for determining how we move and live.<br /><br />This novel captures the struggle of a search. A search for something substantial in a life that if left unattended can become superfluous. The characters in <i>A Home at the End of The World </i> stay active in the search. Alice, Jonathan’s mother, searches for a freedom defined beyond being a couth and staid caregiver. Clare, the third-wheel in a mental polyamorous relationship, searches for a seal of approval on the eccentricities that she has built her life upon. Bobby searches for a family, for a set of warm bodies that won’t leave him to the space of his solitary thoughts. Erich, an afterthought of a character who weasels his way into the novel’s conclusion with unsettled aplomb, searches first for success, and then for the crumbs of a hard-won love. And Jonathan, cosmopolitan-trend-setting Jonathan, he by novel’s end has come to realize that his search has morphed from liberty to confinement, from wanting the world to merely wanting a home.<br /><br />Every day we wake up and crank the car to drive to a job we subconsciously (or vociferously) hate, we too are searching. Searching for our happiness. Searching for our home at the end of the world. A home where questions that are asked are answered. A home where we don’t have to pretend to be something we are not. A home where walls are not there to trap, but are instead there to shelter. A home where every idiosyncrasy, every strange thread in the fabric of who we are, is celebrated and given a value. A home where we are given the license to be whoever we want, do whatever we want, love however we want, go wherever we want, and die whenever we want. I rated this novel a 5 not because I think it is perfection, or that its literary greatness is on par with <i> The Grapes of Wrath </i>, but I rated it a 5 because I have never read a book that captured so seamlessly the essence of this search. It truly and unequivocally has spoken to my soul.<br /><br />But all things existential aside, this novel was good because the writing was beautiful and the characters were real. Even so, this novel is probably not for everyone. At times the plot moves at a leisurely pace, and the shifts of narration (especially the transitions to Alice and Clare) were not always welcomed. Not to mention that its content, which centers on the absurdity of a love between three flawed adults, might find a way to offend the ultra-conservative. However, flaws will be flaws, and if you can look beyond them, I firmly believe you will find something beautiful blossoming in the core of this novel’s pages.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6902170930234242718" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_361892611" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/361892611" />
    <a title="Chaitra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9086236-chaitra"><img alt="Chaitra" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1432611617p2/9086236.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9086236-chaitra?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9086236-chaitra?shelf=made-into-a-movie">made-into-a-movie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9086236-chaitra?shelf=dysfunctional-family">dysfunctional-family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9086236-chaitra?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer361892611" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9919244446905646359">I started out enjoying the book very much. But somewhere along the line, I stopped recognizing motivations of the characters. They're supposed to be unconventional, but I couldn't figure out why they were so. There was something opaque about each of the main trio - Jon, Bobby & Clare. Bobby, to begin with seems a very observant young person. He says of his father buying a Cadillac, that he was as skeptical as his older brother was thrilled. If his father was the kind of person who bought a car o</span>
  <span id="freeText9919244446905646359" style="display:none">I started out enjoying the book very much. But somewhere along the line, I stopped recognizing motivations of the characters. They're supposed to be unconventional, but I couldn't figure out why they were so. There was something opaque about each of the main trio - Jon, Bobby &amp; Clare. Bobby, to begin with seems a very observant young person. He says of his father buying a Cadillac, that he was as skeptical as his older brother was thrilled. If his father was the kind of person who bought a car on a street corner, who knows what else he could do. But Bobby later on is an innocent. He's not just naive, he also seems mostly unaware of what is going around him. It's what I thought of him, anyway.<br /><br />As for Jonathan, I didn't trust that guy one bit. He was a posturer, perhaps by necessity, and it's acknowledged within the story. It makes it hard for other people to get a bead on him, and that makes him a complex person, but unfortunately it was also hard for me as a reader to understand where he's coming from. As a gay man in 80s America, I can understand how hard it must have been for him, but that's just hindsight on my part - had I been ignorant of the persecution and of the AIDS epidemic, I don't think reading Jonathan would make me understand or sympathize for him.<br /><br />I didn't like Clare at all, but I'm not sure we're at all meant to like her. She's the third angle in Bobby and Jon's relationship and for all that Jon says that he loves both of them, Clare ultimately is a nonentity. The other woman in their lives, Jon's mother Alice fairs a lot better, she nurtures both Jon and Bobby and has a voice that is her own. She's one part of the book that I did like. I listened to the book on audio, and it was perfectly fine. I think I made it to the end without zoning out too much on the strength of the narrators - Dallas Roberts (who was my least favorite, but he was still good), Colin Farrell, Jennifer Van Dyck &amp; Jayne Entwistle. I have liked one book by Michael Cunningham, which was not <i>The Hours</i>, so it could be that he's not my type of author. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9919244446905646359" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2445366747" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2445366747" />
    <a title="Ruthiella" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3082480-ruthiella"><img alt="Ruthiella" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1273515825p2/3082480.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2445366747?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ruthiella" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ruthiella" href="/user/show/3082480-ruthiella">Ruthiella</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780140129342" href="/book/show/2207992.A_Home_at_the_End_of_the_World">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2445366747" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18013890722422686068">I felt I needed to give Michael Cunningham another shot since I really didn’t like <u>The Hours</u>. I think thematically there are some similarities here (stifling suburbia being one), but overall I liked <u>A Home at the End of the World</u>, though I don’t think Cunningham is ever going to be a favorite author of mine.<br/><br/>There isn’t a story really but rather the trajectories of four characters who alternately come together and move apart over the space of about 20 years: Alice, the suburban housewife stranded</span>
  <span id="freeText18013890722422686068" style="display:none">I felt I needed to give Michael Cunningham another shot since I really didn’t like <u>The Hours</u>. I think thematically there are some similarities here (stifling suburbia being one), but overall I liked <u>A Home at the End of the World</u>, though I don’t think Cunningham is ever going to be a favorite author of mine.<br /><br />There isn’t a story really but rather the trajectories of four characters who alternately come together and move apart over the space of about 20 years: Alice, the suburban housewife stranded in Cleveland; Jonathan, her gay son who escapes Ohio as soon as he is able; Bobby, Johnathan’s best friend and sometimes lover; and Clare, Johnathan’s best friend/roommate in NYC. I particularly liked the relationship portrayed between Bobby and Jonathan. Also it was interesting to read a book about, at least in part, the AIDS epidemic written during that crisis and not 20 years later with hindsight. Finally, I have only been to NYC once, in the late 90’s but I think that Cunningham got the 80’s aesthetic as a decade for a certain demographic spot on.<br /><br />This would have been a very different book if Cunningham had had only one narrator instead of four. The reader’s sympathies are jostled this way each time the narration shifts. The inner thoughts of the characters, however, were not really distinguishable in tone from the others.<br /><br />5 stars however for introducing me to composer Steve Reich. I've been listening to Variations for Winds, Strings and Keyboards non-stop since finishing the book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18013890722422686068" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3275219606" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3275219606" />
    <a title="Kurt Ostrow" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16931788-kurt-ostrow"><img alt="Kurt Ostrow" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586531885p2/16931788.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3275219606?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kurt Ostrow" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kurt Ostrow" href="/user/show/16931788-kurt-ostrow">Kurt Ostrow</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312202316" href="/book/show/386242.A_Home_at_the_End_of_the_World">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3275219606" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9937830990689608663">"I'd only wanted to hold him for a while, to guide his head to my chest. I'd only wanted to hold on to him as his body went through the long work of giving itself up to the past" (312).<br/><br/>"What I saw was just the wind blowing. It was either the wind or the spirit of the house itself, briefly unsettled by our nocturnal absence but too old to be surprised by the errands born from the gap between what we can imagine and what we in fact create" (336).<br/><br/>Aside from some lite gender essentialism, Cunningham</span>
  <span id="freeText9937830990689608663" style="display:none">"I'd only wanted to hold him for a while, to guide his head to my chest. I'd only wanted to hold on to him as his body went through the long work of giving itself up to the past" (312).<br /><br />"What I saw was just the wind blowing. It was either the wind or the spirit of the house itself, briefly unsettled by our nocturnal absence but too old to be surprised by the errands born from the gap between what we can imagine and what we in fact create" (336).<br /><br />Aside from some lite gender essentialism, Cunningham's novel was a 90s book about life after the 60s that felt deeply modern in 2020, as two men and a woman queerly fashion A HOME AT THE END OF THE WORLD. Cunningham's insights bowled me over on most pages, ranging from the business of opening a restaurant to watching a child grow out of and away from you. <br /><br />Given to me by my friend Moira last year with this inscription: "Twenty-five years ago, my gay older bestie gave me this book for my birthday. It feels right to continue the tradition." May I be so lucky as to keep that tradition alive 25 years from now.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14283528424902051904"><i> “Perhaps, in the extravagance of youth, we give away our devotions easily and all but arbitrarily, on the mistaken assumption that we’ll always have more to give.”</i><br/><br/>3.5/5<br/><br/>This was a beautifully written and evocative novel that had much of the same charm and heart as <i>The Hours</i>, one of my favorites and another novel by Cunningham. This one was split into three parts, and I absolutely loved the first part exploring the childhood of Jonathan and Bobby, but found myself less enchanted with the later pa</span>
  <span id="freeText14283528424902051904" style="display:none"><i> “Perhaps, in the extravagance of youth, we give away our devotions easily and all but arbitrarily, on the mistaken assumption that we’ll always have more to give.”</i><br /><br />3.5/5<br /><br />This was a beautifully written and evocative novel that had much of the same charm and heart as <i>The Hours</i>, one of my favorites and another novel by Cunningham. This one was split into three parts, and I absolutely loved the first part exploring the childhood of Jonathan and Bobby, but found myself less enchanted with the later parts only because there was not much of a plot. I was also sort of upset by the ending. That being said, it was truly gorgeous and I’m glad that I took the time to read it.</span>
